Throughout the list, including beans, barley, flour,
cotton seed, hops, flax, tobacco and hides, the increase
run? fom 16 to 25 per cent in prices. In addition to
this the balance of trade in our favor July I, 1898, was
$615,324,791, for during the year we had exported our
products to the amount of $1,231,329,950, and had im
ported only $616,005,159. So that we sold twice as
much as we bought and took the balance in gold and
liquidated obligations held abroad.
The effect of sound credit and sound money has
been felt throughout all the channels of trade, and has
changed the position of this country to the rest of the
world. Instead of being the world's debtor we are
the creditor nation, with the prospect of increasing
the debts of the nations to us during the next two
years.
J. Ham Lewis has come and gone, like a beautiful
e'ream. He was a lovely figment, but he leaves be
hind the impression that his charms are not for this
practical world. Let him hie to Arcadia and com
pare feathers with the lyre bird, or go down to Ore
gon to compete with the iris and gold of the cock
pheasant from Mongolia, who shames the glories of
the firefly, by the clear-winding Willamette.
BETTER THAN IMPERIALISM.
/â ""* OMMENTING upon the readiness of the peo
t pie to sustain Congress in voting large sums of
money for war purposes and the certainty that
the jingo programme of colonial expansion will entail
even larger expenditures than those caused by the
brief conflict with Spain, the Pittsburg Dispatch points
out that since the nation is rich enough to bear the
burdens of taxation necessary to provide for these ex
penditures, it would be better to apply the money to
internal improvements than to foreign adventures.
The Dispatch says: "The sum of $500,000,000 ex
pended in improving the rivers of the nation, in con
necting the rivers with the lakes and the lakes with
the ocean, would give the entire population between
the Rocky Mountains and the Atlantic coast the
cheapest transportation in the world. A similar sum
expended in establishing irrigation on the arid lands
of the West would add more than 60,000,000 acres to
the productive area of the United States and increase
the wealth of the country by over $1,200,000,000/"
The proposition of course does not come within
the domain of practical politics, but it is worth con
sideration in contrast with the proposed expenditure
of hundreds of millions to establish a colonial system
and educate the mongrel races of the tropics in the
art of self-government.
It is a failing of humanity that both individuals and
nations are more willing to spend money for the sake
of prestige and cutting a big figure in the world than
to provide for comfort at home. Therefore money
for war is more easy to obtain than for internal im
provements.
Nevertheless the Dispatch is right in saying: "The
turning of public attention and public expenditure to
these and cognate questions will give the people of the
United States more real return than all the pchtfMl
for spending money for "imperialism" in savage hikl
antipodal regions.
THE CAMPAIGN IN CALIFORNIA.
WHATEVER defeat and disaster may a waif
them on election day, the fused faction* ol dis
content have at least fulfilled their boftlt of
forcing State issues to the front during the campaign
By nominating for Governor so irresponsible a dema
gogue as James G. Maguire on a platform s<> tht<\U
ening to all the interests of the thrifty and ittduitfioui
classes of the people, they have compelled intelligent
men to recognize that the chief issue in the contest
is that of preserving the State from the domination of
the Maguires and the Gallaghers.
The issue is the more -pressing because the pros
pcrity which followed the accession of McKinlry t<>
office and the speedy enactment of a Republican t.irilT
for the protection of American industry hns resulted
in a renewed activity among capitalists in the direc
tion of large investments and great enterprises. This
has been accompanied by a revival of the westward
march of progressive young men looking for places
in which to establish business and make permanent
homes. California, having vast resources undeveloped
and millions of acres uncultivated, will naturally at
tract many of these home-seekers, but the attraction
will be in vain if the government of the State should
at this juncture be politically unsound and antagonis
tic to capital.
If this had been a year of industrial distress and
financial embarrassment, if thousands of people were
out of work and times were hard, the Maguire move
ment would be everywhere recognized as one of the
most dangerous in our annals. Then its true nature
would have been revealed and its hostility to property
and industry would have shown itself in fierce denun
ciations of all kinds of thrift and all forms of con
servatism. As it is the leaders have had to be some
what mild in their harangues, but none the less they
have said and done enough to exhibit the reckless
character of their policies and their principles. To
defeat them is therefore a necessary step toward our
continuance in the paths of prosperity. It is a State
issue which no property-owner, business man or in
dustrious worker can ignore.
THE SITUATION fIT FASMODA.
GENERAL KITCHENER'S advance guard has
reached Fashoda and found there a French
force under Major Marchand. The British
commander notified the French that Great Britain
claims the territory and demanded their retirement.
Marchand replied courteously that France claims the
territory, and in turn demanded that the British retire.
The incident closed for the time with the opposing
forces encamped within view of each other; there to
remain on friendly terms until the diplomats of Lon
don and Paris decide which shall retire.
The situation is strikingly like, one which occurred
between the forces of the same nations when they
were struggling for supremacy in this country during
the middle of the last century. The British colonists
along the Atlantic had extended their outposts to the
crests of the Alleghenies, and claimed the Ohio River
region beyond. A French force marched down from
Canada and 9tarted to establish a fort at the point
where Pittsburgh now stands. George Washington,
then serving in the British army as a major of the
Virginia colonial troops, was sent forward to warn
the daring Frenchmen to vacate the territory, and did
so, coming up with their advance guard some dis
tance from the site where the fort was in process of
construction.
Up to that point the incident in America 150 years
ago was almost exactly like that in Africa to-day. The
results of the meeting of the two forces in the last cen
tury, however, were very different from those which
have followed from the meeting at Fashoda. There
was no courteous interchange of compliments and
patient waiting for diplomatists in the old days. Wash
ington attacked the French as soon as he met them,
and the fight that followed was the beginning of a war
that did not end until the French were driven out of
not only the Ohio River region, but Canada as well.
It will be seen the civilized world has made a long
stride since the last century. Nations are no longer
to be hurried into wars by the rash actions of com
manders of distant outposts. The pen of the diplo
matist in the capital dominates the sword of the war
rior even in the wilderness of barbarous lands, and
compels armies to halt in the face of one another in
stead of fighting as soon as they meet.
Tennyson, when in a fit of pessimism he wrote
"Maud," denounced with no little indignation this
curbing of the military valor of men, and said with
scorn: "The jingling of the guinea helps the hurt
that honor feels; and the nations do but murmur,
snarling at each other's heels." Few intelligent men,
however, will regret the change that has come over
the world. The incident at Fashoda will not be so
heroic a record on the pages of history as that which
took place on the frontier of Pennsylvania in the last
century, but it will none the less be a proof of the ad
vancing civilization of both the British and the
French.
Even the Memphis Commercial-Appeal takes up
the warfare against the First Tennessee stationed at
this point. It should bear in mind that in this regi
ment are about 150 untamed moonshiners, caught in
the Southern mountains, and never before brought
into contact with any civilization save that represent
ed by revenue officers. The rest of the regiment is
made up of as good blood as has gone to the front,
and the officers are gentlemen to whom the conduct
of the few scalawags has caused greater pain than it
could have caused to any one else.
There is no use in jumping to the conclusion that
California volunteers want to be mustered out of the
service, nor that the purpose for which they were
called to arms has been fully accomplished. On the
other hand, some who are being mustered out do not
accept the situation joyously, and there is no assur
ance that grave work does not await in the Philip
pines.
Charles Van Alstine, a returned Klondiker, who
married on a few hours' acquaintance, is reported as
"alarmed" because his wife has disappeared with most
of his ready cash. There is not the slightest occasion
for alarm or even surprise. While his belief that there
hag been foul play is well founded, Charles may be
reasonably assured that he is the only victim of it.
It is a fortunate circumstance that Aguinaldo should
have the columns of a New York paperthrough which
to address the people of the <vorld. It gives some
space writer the assurance of being able to look the
landlady in the face.
At last there seems to be a growing impression that
the Spanish must get out of Cuba. If recollection is
not at fault, American troops were sent there under
the stimulus of the same idea.
Highwaymen in the Klondike have begun to rob
and murder. Thus do the influences of a benign civ
ilization reach into and permeate the Arctic circle.

How is that?" 4>38
"Well," replied Delany, "the property
that we want to lease is that now occu

firm."
"Supervisor Devany?" queried the
Mayor.
"Yes," it is rented by Devany & Hop
kins from Dr. Ragan of the School
Board," replied the captain. "The build-
Ing is to be refitted at Ragan's expense.
The lease will provide that.
Subsequently there was some explana
tion demanded by the reporters. There
upon Delany made this statement: "We
do not consider the rent too high, in view
of the fact that Ragan will have to ex
pend $3000 on the building to put it in
proper condition for use as a police sta
tion."
This statement was somewhat mystify
ing in view of the fact that $3000 expended
by Dr. Ragan, out of two years' rental,
would only leave him $984 profit, rather
a small sum. considering the location of
the property and the size of the build
ing, 60 by 100 feet.

Phelan's remark after the meeting ad
journed. "Property out in that neigh
borhood ought not to rent at such a fig
ure." It Is highly probable that Super
visor Delany will be called upon by the
Mayor to explain which is getting the
worst of the deal, the city or Ragan.

terests in the lease, alleging that Hopkins
and himself merely rented of Ragan and

8 HORSES ON
Â« THE
B COLONEL.
$ .
CfOOfOOOOOOO had sen adventure
which has advanced him three numbers
among the Palace Guards, of which Colo
nel Chadbourne is . senior officer. The
usual Saturday, night assemblage was in
session, and no .matter what was said in
the way of risque stories. Colonel Doo
llttle maintained a deep silence. Some one
noticed that his mind was far away and
made mention of it. The colonel came
out of his trance and told his tale:
"Gentlemen of the unspotted brigade,
whose honor has never been questioned,
my Badness is due to horses. Oh, no, I
have not been to the races. Worse, I
have been investing in horse-flesh. I
was about to make a trip up the coun
try a few days ago and I wanted to buy
a horpe for my own use. A friend ad
vised me to go to the stock yards, where
I would certainly get one very cheap. I
went, and he went with me. There the
horses stood around with numbers plas
tered, on their backs â skates- with every
variety of disorder known to Veterinary
experts. The catalogue of diseases which
Petruchio recites- in 'Taming of the
Shrew' were all materialized. Out of the
lot I noticed one horse with number 17
on his side. He was sound in wind and
beautiful of form, and I determined to
buy- him when the auction began. Soon
after the sale on number 17 was called
and I stripped for work. Ten dollars, $20,
$30, up .it went, and before I knew where
I was I had offered $80 for the horse. It
Eeemed to me that this was pretty stiff,
but I never like to be beat, and I stayed
with It. Finally I won at 190; the rest
had â  dropped out. â  .
"I felt pretty good, and after the sale
I went over to the auctioneer to see about
the delivery of the horse. He directed
me to one of the corrals. I asked for
number 17, and ten men. each leading a
skate, rushed up to me.. I had bought a
lot of useless horse-flesh. You see num
ber 17 was a lot and I got the entire
works. What will I do with them? I
don't know^ That's what- makej me
sad."

A PENSIONER'S TVIDOW-^J. C. T..
City. The widow of a soldier who was
honorably discharged from the service of
the United States in time of peace, on
account of disabilities incurred in such
service, and who was allowed a pension
by reason of, such disability, is entitled to
her pension, the same as if her late hua
band had served in one of the wars.
CTRCUJÂ«*JAVIGATION-C. S.. City. No
one has as yet circumnavigated the ex
treme' north coast of the North American
continent. There have been more than
two hundred- expeditions to search for the
northwest' passage. It was discovered by
several, notably, Franklin, Hudson and
Baffin. Greely and others have reached
very far north, but not far enough to dis
cover "a clear passage from the Atlantic
to the Pacific." â 
LAFAYETTE'S WATCH â Subscriber,
City. The Congress of the United States
did not purchase a watch for General La
fayette, but took one out of pawn for res
toration to his family. On t>e occasion
of Lafayette's visit to the United States
in 1825, his pocket' was picked of a watch
that had been presented to him by Wash
ington. Forty-eight- years after the
stolen watch was found In a pawnshop
in New York City and on the 22d of June,
1874, Congress, by joint resolution, ordered
the purchase of the watch and its restora
tion to Lafayette's family.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, SEPTEMBER 27, 1893.
DROWNED IN THE BREAKERS.
â f While trying to make a landing at Rockport In a small boat yesterday it capsized and Captain Hansen â¦
+ and a sailor named Englund were drowned. Another sailor got ashore on the bottom of the overturned +
+ boat and a fourth was saved with a life line. +
â¦ â¦
DEATHS AND
CASUALTIES
OS THE FRONT
Captain and Sailor of
the Scotia Drowned.
FATALITY ON THE BLAIRMORE
A CHIPPER KILLED BY A FALL
INTO THE HOLD.
Arrival of the Steam Whaler ITew
port Prom the Arctic â She
Brings No Additional
News.
It Tvas "blue Monday" on the water
front yesterday. Four deaths, several
accidents and a number of captains seri
ously ill was the record for the day, and
it has not been equaled for a long time.
Two of the deaths were by drowning,
one was from falling down the hold of
a ship, and the other due to natural
causes. All in all it was a day that will
not soon be forgotten.
The steam schooner Scotia left here on
the 21st inst. for Rockport, Meniloeino
County, and arrived there the next day.
It was too rough to load, so Captain
Hansen stood oft and on with his vessel
and filially managed to make fast to the
mooring buoy last Sunday. The southeast
er that blew here Saturday night worked
its way up the coast, and yesterday it
suddenly became very rough at Kock
port. In order to save his vessel Captain
Hansen hau to slip hTs inoorings and put
to sea.
It was very rough, but the captain was
quite anxious to get word to his owners
in this city, so he had a boat lowered
and v.ith three seamen started in it for
the beach. Before half the distance hail
been covered the boat capsized and Cap
tain Frithof. Hansen and a sailor, sup
posed to be V. Euglund, were drowned.
Another sailor reached the capsized boat
and was washed ashore on it. The fourth
man swam into the breakers and the
people of Rockport, who were all on the
beach, managed to get a line to him and
he was dragged ashore.
When the boal capsized the Scotia
stood out to sea, and last night was
about two miles off shore, waiting for the
captain to return. The supposition is
that Captain Hansen wanted one or the
other of his mates to take the message
ashore, and when they refused on ac
count of the weather he undertook the
dangerous task himself.
The above particular* were received by
telephone from Rockport. The following
was sont to the Merchants' Exchange:
Capiain Hansen was formerly a quar
termaster in the employ of the Pacific
Coast Steamship Company. Then he
became mate of the Scotia, but last Jan
uary was made master. He was a very
careful- man, and knew the coast well.
He leaves a widow and six children.
Another death took place on the Blair
more yeÃterday. Over two years ago the
bark capsized in Mission bay and several
of the officers and men were drowned.
When the vessel was raised the owners
and underwriters began fighting over the
insurance money, and It was only a month
or so ago that the matter was settled
The Blairmore was then sold and docked
at "Harrison street wharf to be re-rigged
and fitted out. Yesterday while several
men were chipping the rust from the in-
Klde of the vessel an elderly chipper
known as "Captain" Edward Gregg, lost
hjs balance and fell into the lower hold
He died before a physician could reach
Michael Fey had an ankle broken and
was seriously injured otherwise at Tele
graph Hill yesterday. He was getting
rock out for ship's ballast when a Wg
piece fell on him. He was treated by Drs.
Hill and Zabala at the Harbor Receiving
Hospital iiml was then sent home.
Martin Johnson, iirst mate of the
schooner Albert Meyer, met with a very
serious accident yesterday. The vessel s
cargo of sugar had been discharged and
she was being prepared for sea. The
anchor wa.s being catted and Johnson
was helping the sailors. Suddenly the
vessel gave a roll and the fishhook that
\\; ; s holding the anchor slipped. The an
chor went overboard and the fishhook
lU-w back, and, striking Johnson in the
face knocked him Benseless. It was
nearly three hours before he knew what
had struck him, while his face looked afl
though he had been beaten with a club.
Thomas Reardon, a s-witchman on the
Belt railroad, had his foot badly crushed
early yesterday morning. He was switch
ing some cars, opposite Lombard-street
wharf, when ho slipped on the w^t pavf
ment and fell with one foot on the track.
One of the cars passed over the foot,
li'ith Reunion and Johnson were treated

Captain Mclntyre is very sick on board
his ship, the Clan Graham, and Captain
Phillips of the May field has been removed

ment.

CELEBRATED
THE DAY OF
ATONEMENT
Observed With Fast
ing and Prayer.
SYNAGOGUES WERE CROWDED
RABBIS DELIVER MOST ELO
QUENT SERMONS.
Devotional Services Mark the Most
Solemn and Sacred Day
of the Jewish
Year.
The most sacred and solemn day of the
Jewish year, Yom Klppur, was observed
yesterday with full ceremonies in all the
synagogues. It was the day of atonement
and fasting and the solemnity of the
services held the worshipers from the
early morning till the setting of the sun.
The Yom Kippur services of Sunday
night ushered in the prayers and fasting
which continued until 6 o'clock last night.
The choirs of the Temple Emanu-El and
Sherlth Israel were augmented and the
beautiful and soul-stirring music of the
services at both synagogues was exquis
itely ' rendered. The white drapery, the
dim lights and the flowers lent solemnity
to the congregations of worshipers ab
sorbed in the devotional observance of
the day.
At the Temple Emanu-El every seat
was occupied from the beginning of the
services to the last prayer. Rabbi Jacob
Yocrsanger lectured early in the after
noon on a subject consonant with the
import of the day. His words were full
of warning and carried to the hearts of
his auditors the great blessings that came
from a life of religion, piety and good
aese in the liberal een.se of the words.
Rabbi Kieto of Sherlth Israel spoke
eloquently on the meaning of the day of
atonement. "The True Jew" was his
theme, which he treated in eloquent and
masterful style. He closed hia oration
with an exhortation to his hearers to look
well into their lives and strive to keep
In the paths of virtue and righteousness
and shun the habits of sin.
Rabbi M. S. Levy lectured at the noon
hour before an audience that fairly
packed the Geary-street Temple. He re
viewed the history of Judaism and ex
plained why the day of atonement should
be observed with full solemnity. Ilia
words were replete with religious elo
quence and went straight to the hearts
of his congregation.
The memorial services which be#an late
In the* afternoon at all the houses of
worship wre most impressive. The
names of those who died during the year
were mentioned and special prayers deliv
ered for them.
HER DAUGHTERS PERJURY.
idmission of Mrs. Tread-well's Moth-
er in the Reay Suit.
The old suit of J. M. Reay against
Mabel Treadwell, individually as execu
trix of the will of J. P. Treadwell; C. G.
Warren, as administrator of the estate of
John Butler and Arthur Inkersley, as
special administrator of the estate of P.
H. Owens, in which the plaintiff seeks to
recover titlo and possession of some fifty
acres of land in the. Dana tract, near the
old Lone Mountain Cemetery, is now be
ing heard by Judge Hebbard.
In 1862, the plaintiff .alleges, .he pur
chased the property from P. H. Owens
Subsequently J. P. Treadwell, at the head
of an armed following, drove off the em
ployes of the plaintiff and destroyed the
Improvements. The other defendants held
and still claim adverse titles.
The suit has occupied the attention of
the courts for decades. Shortly after
Treadwell's death the action was heard
in part. During the hearing it developed
that an affidavit of Frederick William
had been abstracted from the papers In
the case. Mrs. Treadwell was suspected
of having taken the document and was
summoned to appear and testify regard
ing its loss. She stated that she knew
nothing of the document, and the trial
progressed. Yesterday was the first day
of the second hearing. Mrs. Katherine
Summers, mother of Mrs. Treadwell, wÂ»s
placed upon the stand and made some ad
missions that would have produced a pro
found sensation had Mrs. Treadwell been
alive to answer. Mrs. Summers testified
that her daughter uiq !Â».:â "â Â«Â» the document
in her possession during the first hear
ing of the case, and misled the court when
she stated otherwtae. Mrs. Summers also
testified that during the first hearing of
the ease, when the testimony of Leopold
Markgraff was rmtch wanted by the plain
tiff, she saw her daughter go to Con
Crowley, give him some money and tell
him to Mil Markgraff up with whisky, so
that he would be unable to attend the
trial. Markgraff was an important wit
ness for the plaintiff, and the admissions
of Mrs. Summers will greatly strengthen
his case. The case goes on to-day.
STREET BAILBOAD BONDS SOLD.
Los Angeles Capitalists Dispose of
Some of Their Stock in San
Francisco.
Quite a bunch of the Los Angeles street
railroad bonds change hands yesterday In
this city. George W. Baker, an attorney
acting for N. K. Hasten, a San Fran
cisco capitalist, bought one-seventh of the
bonds or the railroad company at 80 cents
on the dollar. This is a greater price than
they have ever brought before. At this
figure Masten secured $220,000 worth nt
$176,000.
The transfer was not unanimous as
two of the trustees voted against dis
posing of the bonds, but the majority
ruled, and Baker secured what he wanted
for his client.

HE CIME OUT
FROM THE EAST
TO BE DUPED
Another of Mrs. Grace
Allison's Victims.
STRANDED IN A BIG CITY
PITIFUL TALE OF A GB-AY
HAIRED OLD GENTLEMAN.
He Came Here Expecting to Wed the
"Wealthy Widow," but Was
Soon Heartlessly Dis
carded.
The police are occasionally hearing from
dupes of Mrs. Grace Allison, the "wealthy
widow" of Bain's matrimonial agency,
and yesterday one of them, an old man
with white chin whiskers and wearing a
pair of gold-rimmed spectacles, called at
police headquarters and told his tale of
woe.
His name Is Alexander S. Sampson, and
he came all the way from West Duxbury.
Plymouth County, Mass., to wed the
wealthy widow. He arrived here about
six weeks ago and brought with him only
$25, tvs he expected to get married at once
to the alleged widow and live comfortably
off her wealth. She saw him, and after
learning that he was only possessed of
a smrU! ;>iece of property in West Dux
bury she discarded him. His money soon
ran out, and he had to search for work
to keep him alive.
"I joined the matrimonial agency," he
said, "last December and start*Q to cor
respond with Mrs. Allison. The letters
were few at first, but toward March they
came more frequently, and I sent her my
photograph. I asked her to send hers in
return, but she wrote me that she did
not have one, and asked me to send her
$5 to get them taken and she would send
me one. Being anxious to see how she
looked I sent her the money, but did not
receive a photograph. The letters then
dropped off again, but I finally wrote her
that I was coming to marry her. and she
replied that she and Mr. Curtis would
meet me at the Sixteenth-street station,
Oakland.
"They did not meet me, but the day af
ter my arrival I called at the agency and
three times at her house on Jones street,
later. She received me cordially at first,
but after she found out that I was not a
wealthy man she would have nothing to
do with me."
Sampson brought with him all the let
ters written to him by Mrs. Allison, and
some of them are gems. They will be
taken to the postal authorities to-day, as
there is a good case against her for us
ing the mails to obtain money by fraud.
Mrs. Allison is still in the City Prison
as a prisoner of the United States Mar
shal, and Sergeant Moflitt took the old
man to the prison to see her. She is in
a cell with some other women, and the
old man at once picked her out.
"Don't you know me?" he asked her.
"No, I don't," she snapped. "I never
saw you before, you old fool. Say, girls,
what would I do with an old guy like
that?" and the women joined In her
laugh.

COMMERCIAL WORLD.
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver unchanged.
Wheat and Barley quiet.
Oats in Rood demand.
Corn and Rye still dull.
Hay unaffected by the rain.
Fair export of Beans.
Dried Peas lower.
Potatoes and Onions wrak.
Vegetables continue plentiful.
Butter and cheese steady.
Another advance in Eggs.
More Eastern Poultry in.
Cranberries in free supply and lower.
Wine Grapes advancing.
Peaches, Pears and Plums dull.
Raisins active and firm.
Dried Fruits continue quiet.
Nothing new in Provisions.
Wool, Hops and Hides unchanged.
Hugs continue weak.
FRUIT AND SALMON EN ENGLAND.
Th* London Grocers' Gazette Bays: The firm
feeling In Ml: n noted in our recent i^su-s
continues, and prices Bhow a further advance
of about is per case for the ek, the tnta.l
advance on Fraser River salmon being about
C* per case, "combine" prices as a basis. While
business has not i-een particularly brisk, buy
ers are more eager than sellers; in fact, for
anything approaching a fair "line" of salmon
an udvanee above current rates Is wanted. For
the new season there is no business passing,
the opinion of packers being that top prices
have not yet been nearly reached, and conse
quently for the moment they are not sellers.
California fruits continue scarce and dear.

WEATHKR CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The storm is to-night central Jn Washington,
moving slowly eastward. The barometer has
risen over California, Nevada and Southern
-.. and Is falling to the east of the Rocky
Mountains.
The temperature has fallen decidedly over
Nevada, Western Oregon and Eastern Call
f. rnla. It has risen in Utah.
The weather is cloudy throughout the Pacific
Elope and rain is now falling ln Nevada and
Southeastern California. The rain to-day has
inflned to Nevada and California north
of Tehachapi. The rain will extend Into South
ern California to-night and Tuesday morning.

hours ending midnight, Sept. 27, IS9S :

southwest Wind.

NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, Sept. 26.â action of to
day's market at the opening was disappointing
to the bulls, and stocks purchased Saturday
on tri" favorable bank statement were speedily
let go and commitments made for the short
account. The higher range of London prices
encouraged the bulls, and efforts were made
to advance prices to a parity with the Lon
don figures. The rise In the foreign market
for Union Pacific showed that foreigners were
becoming reconciled to the dividend action. |
Opening prices were Por"â hat mixed, but the j
hesitancy waa soon succeeded by a general
plunge downward under the guidance of the .
specialties. St n loss orders were uncovered in ;
the leaders, and the market for a time was at !
the mercy of the dealers who searched dili
gently for weak spots Gossip that disagree- \
ment existed between some of the speculative
pools was i lored to some extent by the sever- I
lty of the break in spots. Sugar, after a dip
downward of a point, encountered supporting
orders and ruled above Saturday's close for
the remainder of the day. The railways mean
while tell Bympathelcally, but the losses were ;
confined t" fractions, and after the first rush '
to sell had abated settled down to a level
slightly above the lowest, and resisted subse
quent efforts at depression. The firmness was !
acounted for by the publication of numerous
trnfftc statements, covering a wide area of ter
ritory and showing substantial Increases. The
elimination of an active speculative interest |
from the market owing to the rlgfd observance I
of the Hebrew holiday, Yom Klppur, reduced
business consfrt*' >
Sugar moved up to 123 and the railways ruled
the best. The market left off unsettled, with
Blight losses generally. Call money rates stiff
ened sharpl- late In the day and 5% per cent
was quoted for a time. The great bulk, how
ever, of the transactions were made at 3% per
cent, and some accommodations were secured
early in the day at 3 per cent.
The bond markr-t lacked decisive tone, but
dealings were lls,'ht and variations in either '
direction unimportant. Total sales, $1,329,000.
Government bonds were unchanged on bid
prices, but a purchase of the new fours cou- ,
pon was made at *& higher than the last sale. j

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
', CHICAGO, Sept. Wheat Bhowed consld
| erable weakness at the start. Both foreign and
domestic news was of a bearish nature. Liver- '
pool Ehowed %@l%d decline and Paris was oft
:15 centimes. Receipts .here were 312 cars, of â¢
: which 33 were of contract grade. Besides this
! about 200,000 bushels of contract wheat was re
ceived by lake and out of private houses. Min
neapolis ana Duluth reported 1663 cars, against
! 1993 last- week and 1427 a year ago. Before the
market had fairly started weakness began to
develop in cash wheat in the Northwest, which
had Its effect here. The market touched 63% c
for a moment, but selling pressure was too
much for the very moderate demand and prices
slowly sagged oil. For the first hour or so
1 nothing occurred to check the bearishness, ex
cept an indication that the visible supply state
| ment might show a decrease instead oi show
ing 1,000,000 buthels increase as expected. .Li
quidation became less urgent after that and
the market rallied to Â«3Vic. No more fluctua
tions of note occurred until' after the visible
supply statement came out. This was a sur
prise, showing a decrease of SSO.OOO bushels,
and an instant change in the tone of the mar
ket was noticed. Professional shorts and early
sellers rushed for cover and found considerable
dlfliculty in satisfying their demands. Another
I feature in the strength was rumors of heavy
I export engagements, the statement being- made
that 100 loads had been disposed of late Sat
'â â  urday night and to-day. These figures were
disputed by shippers, but had their effect
nevertheless. Primary receipts amounted to
1,792,000 bushels; amount on ocean passage in
creased 2.450.000 bushels. The market became
; very strong toward the close, trading increas
j ing greatly In volume and a substantial ad
vance in prices took place. December closed
%c higher.
Corn was slow until near the close, when It
developed considerable strength. December
closed Me higher.
Oats were dull and uninteresting until near
; the close, when. in sympathy with wheat and
corn, the market became fairly active and
firm. May closed a Bhade higher.
Provisions were fairly active. Yellow fever
i â news and liberal hog receipts were responsible
j for weakness at the opening and lower prices
i prevailed for some time. Demand from pack
ers and shorts l<ecame fairly heavy later, and,
offerings falling off, prices advanced rapidly.
January pork closed 15c higher. January lard
7%c higher and January ribs 7%c higher.

DECIDUOUS AND CITRUS FRUITS.
Cranberries are coming in heavily and are
lower.
Wine Grapes are doing better and there are
good prospects for a further advance, as the
crop seems to be getting shorter all the time.

Plums are scarce, but dull, as there is very
little demand for them. Peaches and Pears
are also dull.

DRIED FRUITS, . UTS, RAISINS, ETC.
The New York Commercial says, under dftte
of September 20: "There Is some increase In
activity in California raisins, due to the an
nounced purpose of the association to advance
prices Â»4c after October S. Supplies for Sep
tember shipment are exhausted, and most buy
ers are purchasing for October, but no rates
are possible beyond October 8. The association
has said that rates would probably go up at
that time, and unleaa the declaration Is modi
fled, purchases for October will be compara
tively small until after the change. It was
said yesterday, however, that perhaps 20 cars
had been taken for October shipment at pres~
ent figures, though the statement was uncon
firmed.
"Commission men are not entirely satisfied
with their treatment by the association, but
few care to express their views openly. They
feel, however, that too .much uncertainty has
prevailed, and even now they do not know
whether the association will advance prices
or not. The latest Information Is that If tht
officers think best prices will be advanct-d.
But that Is too Indefinite to Insure steady busi
ness or satisfactory dealing in any form.
"There Is nothing; new In prunes. Prices are
unchanged, and trade Inclined to be moderate.
Demand is , largely confined to large sizes, of
which there are none â¢ procurable. Medium
sizes are all the trade can get, and there Is
no prospect of any change in the situation.
Futures attract little Interest, even In the face
of short crop reports, which are being con
firmed dally."
The local market Is quiet and unchanged.

THti STOCK MAKKJDT.
Lower prices ruled for mining stocks, with a
quiet business.
Trading in local securities was smaller than
for some time and quotations showed no
changes worthy of note.
At special meetings of stockholders yesterday
a reduction of the capitalization of the Crown
Point Mining Company was authorized from
$100 per share to $3 and of the Julia Consoli
dated Mining Company from $100 to $1 per
share.
Special meetings of the stockholders of the
Exchequer, Silver Hill and Con. New York
mining companies will be held to-day to au
thorize a reduction in their capitalization from
$100 to $1 per share.
The South Swansea Mining Company of Utah
has declared a dividend of Be per share,
amounting to $7500.
The Geyser-Marlon Mining Company of Utah
has declared a dividend of 2c per share,
amounting to $6000. .
The Mountaineer Mill and Mining Company
of Nevada County has levied an assessment of
2c per share, delinquent October 21.
The Live Oak Consolidated Gold Mining
Company has levied an assessment of 10c per
share, delinquent October 20.

rail from the mines of Eureka and Hamilton
districts, Nevada, for the week ending Sep
tember 23: EureJca" Districtâ Eureka Con., 94,

N. Westhelmer, managing director of the
Standard Consolidated Mining Company, says:
"While In accumulating our cash one or more
dividends may have been held In the treasury,
we believe In this conservative manner of pro
viding through the means of a larger capital
In the future for still greater results, and we
are thus enabled to acquire new properties of
value if such present thÂ»mselveÂ« to us, and
having on September 1. I<WS, $80,000 cash on
hand, with but our usual output, we will have
by January 1, 1R99, besides providing for an
other dividend, $100,000 ensh on hand, thus hav
ing paid three dividends of 10c each on a Rtook
soiling under $1 75 and our mines in excellent
shape for future work, besides our large sur
plus -to secure our position. During the past
year our able superintendent has opened up
several new producing points, from which we
hope to get extensive ore."

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographlo
Office, located in the Merchants' Exchange, Is
maintained in San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
Jights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of Interest to ocean commerce.
The time bn.ll on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street. Is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Signal
Observatory. Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any, Is published
in the morning papers the following day.
CHAS. P. WELCH,
Ensign (retired), U. S. N., In charge.

FRIENDS DO
NOT BELIEVE
IN SUICIDE
Gregoire's Death Yet
a Mystery.
STORY OF A VOLUNTEER
HEARD ANGRY WORDS PRIOR
TO THE SHOOTING.
The Position of the Gun Suggests
That the Deceased Did Not
Use It on Him
self.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Sept. 26.
Friends of Cleophas Gregoire are nof at
all satisfied that he committed suicide.
The evidenoe placed before the Coroner's
Jury did not succeed in convincing that
body that Cleophas Gregoire died by his
own hand, and there are many circum
stances which indicate that he may have
been murdered.
Captain Anderson, of the Volunteers of
America, gave testimony which needs
some explanation, and the police should
certainly make an effort to discover the
man with whom Gregoire was quarreling
just twenty minutes before he came to
his death in such a mysterious manner.
Captain Anderson testified as follows:
"I left the Methodist church about ten
minutes to 10 o'clock with my wife and
her sister, who lives on Twenty-second
street. We walked down to Twenty-sec
ond street, and at the corner of Twenty
second and Grove we stopped and talked
as my wife's eister had only a block to
go .we went no further with her. On the
corner there stood two men talking. They
did not seem to agree. I paid particular
attention to them, and on the other side
there stood another man; he stood still
there. I turned and looked back at these
men that were talking there, and as I
looked at them they walked up toward
San Pablo and Grove. We talked there I
guess for ten minutes. When we came
back again, I and my wife, I noticed this
man that Is down In the Morgue now
was standing on the corner of San Pablo
avenue and Hobart street talking to a
man that was not so tall as this man
On the other side the same man was
standing across the street that was
standing down on Twenty-second street
I positively identify this man lying in the
Morgue as being one of those men. They
were talking about a contract. But the
car came along and made a noise and I
did not hear all the conversation. It oc
curred to me that it was a pretty dan
gerous place, people standing on the cor
ner talking like this, and I went on "
In response to the question "Who -was
this third man that you have described as
f-tanding off to one side?" the witness
answered: "He was standing on the other
side of the street. He had an overcoat
on. I could see that across the street
when the car passed." The witness went
on to describe the clothing -of the men
and the angry manner In which they were
talking.
"Did you notice the attitude of this de- i
ceased man?," was BJÂ»ked- the w!tnw.Â«.--
"Yes; the other man was talking to 'â 
this man of something that this other!
man wanted of this man (the deceased)
or something that he wanted him to con
sent to. That is what It seemed as I !
passed by."
There Is also much curiosity expressed
as to how, presuming Gregoire committed
suicide, the pistol should be found under
his shoulder while his hands were at his
side. The general experience of police
officers has been that when a man com
mits suicide the pistol is either close by
his hand or firmly clutched within the
lingers. Officer Swain testified that he
found the pistol underneath the deceased
between his right elbow and. shoulder.
The testimony of Mr. Remlllard showed
that during the day the deceased was
making all possible arrangements to
leave for Canada, and no evidence has
yet besn brought forward to suggest a
reason for suicide, â 
Some surprise has been expressed that
Dr. Perkins, who was the man who dis
covered the dead body of . Gregoire on the
grass, was not called at the inquest. In
describing the manner in which he found
Gregoire to a reporter Dr. Perkins said,
"Most decidedly this is not a case of sui
cide." â¢

Prisoners Observe Atonement Day.
The twelve convicts who are of the Jew
ish faith now serving sentence In San
Quentin observed the Day of Atonement
within the prison walls. These men
strictly observed the fast from sunset on
Sunday until sunset yesterday, when they
were treated to a sumptuous dinner by
the prison authorities, purlng the twen
ty-four hours of the fast religious service
consisting of prayers and reading of the
scriptures was observed.

PUGS CROSS THE COUNTY LINE.
A Carload of San Francisco Boughs
Enter ban Mateo to Fight.
A sufficient number of San Francisco
roughs to fill an electric car of the San
Francisco and San Mateo line left this
city last night about 9 o'clock and crossed
the county line into San Mateo, where
they indulged in a prize fight with bare
knuckles.
The news of their departure from San
Francisco came in by the way of Ocean
View over the telephone wire with but
meager information, stating that "Kid"
Johnston and a man named McMahon
had been matched to fight ten rounds
Following this set-to Edward Prender
gast of Ocean View was to face a New
York bruiser named Collins in a like
number of rounds. The motive which
prompted these fights is said to be the
settlement of some old grudges existing
between the quartet. The exact place
where the mill came off was carefully
kept secret, fearing the information
might reach the Sheriff of San Mateo
County.
The San Francisco police had been
posted on the movements of the roughs,
but their jurisdiction ended at the county
line and it was too late to telegraph the
Sheriff of the adjoining county in time
to Btop the fight.

EIGHT HARD ROUNDS.
Eugene Mulligan Won a Decision
From the vlame Charley John-
son on Points.
ThÂ« Alpine Athletic Club gave its
monthly entertainment last evening, and
a crowded house nleased the managers.
The two events that ushered in the
event of the evening could not be consid
ered appetizers, as they were, so to speak,
as tame as church mice. In fact, the ser
geant of police in attendance was serious
ly inclined to stop the alleged boxing, as
the spectators were suffering an intense
tired feeling.
The feature of the evening, however,
made up for the opening events. It was
an eight-round contest between the game
and willing auburn-haired pugilist Eugene
Mulligan of the Merchants' Athletic Club
and Charles Johnson of the California
Athletic and Swimming Club. The bout
was thoroughly Interesting, as the con
testants fought every inch of ground from
start to finish, and in the last round the
contest was of such a spirited nature that
every spoft present was on his leet and
shouting for his favorite.
In the opening round Mulligan scored a
lead until near the finish, when Johnson
opened his dog's of war* and landi-d a few
heavy right-handers on "Mull's" chin
which partially dazed the recipient.
The second ended with honors even. In
the third , MulliKan landed four hard
rights on Johnson's left ear and two body
blows, which were very effective. This
was Mulligan's round beyond question.
The fourth was replete with hard
smashes, both men landing blow for blow.
Johnson retired to his corner looking very
red In the face. Johnson had a shade the
best of the fifth.
The sixth was Mulligan's on points, as
he repeatedly ducked very cleverly under
Johnson's right-hand jabs and time and
again he put his right on Johnson's ear.
According to Queensberry rules a clever
duck scores in favor of the ducker and
against the man who falls to land. This
is something which referees seldom take
into consideration.
The seventh round also ended in favor
of Mulligan on points, as Johnson seem
ingly relied solely on his good right to
put Mulligan out, but repeatedly failed
to land. Had he used his left on "straight
leads and then placed his right on the jaw
when a favorable opportunity presented
a different result might have been an
nounced.
The eighth terminated slightly In favor
of Johnson, who had the better staving
qualities and fought like a demon. Both
men were very groggy when the gong
sounded for the end of hostilities. Pos
sibly if the contest had lasted ten rounds
Johnson would have won, as he evidently
had the better staying qualities.

The entertainment and reception com

Friday evening Leo XIII booth. Miss

dally journal, styled St. Patrick's Bazaar
Bulletin, during the fair.
Mrs. 11. J. Hanchette Dead.
Mrs. Emma A. Hanchette, a school
teacher of Los Angeles, died at the Lane
Hospital yesterday forenoon from the re-
Bult of a surgical operation.
The death of Mrs. Hanchette recalls the
mysterious disappearance of her husband,
Harry Jay Hanchette, about ten years
ago, since â which time he has never been
heard from. At the time of his dlsap
pearanoe he left this city to go to Los
Angeles, where everything was then
booming. From that day to the present
he has never been seen by any one who
knew him.

PROCLAMATION.
6tate of california.
executive: department,
SACRAMENTO, July 30th, 1898.
WHEREAS, The Legislature of the Stute of
California, at Its thirty-second session begin-
ning on the fourth day of January, A. V. 1897,
two-thirds of all the members elected to earn
of the two houses of said Legislature voting
In favor thereof, proposed the following de-
scribed amendments to the Constitution of the
State of California, to-wlt:
AMENDMENT NUMBER ONE.
(Being Senate Constitutional Amendment No.
41.)
A resolution to propose to the people of the
State of California an amendment to section
eighteen of article eleven of the Constitu-
tion, in relation to revenue and taxation, by
which it is proposed to amend said section to
read as follows:
Bection 18. No county, city, town, township,
board of education or school district shall in-
cur any indebtedness or liability In any man-
ner or for any purpose exceeding in any year
the income and revenue provided for it for
such year, without the absent of two-thirds
of the qualified electors thereof, voting at an
election to be held for that purpose, nor un-
less before, or at the time of incurring such
indebtedness, provision shall be made for the
collection of an annual tax sufficient to pay
the Interest on such Indebtedness as It falls
due, and also provision to constitute a sinking
fund for the payment of the principal thereof
on or before maturity, which shall not exceed
forty years from the time of contracting the
same; provided, however, that the City and
County of San Francisco may at any time pay
the unpaid claims with interest thereon for
materials furnished to' and work done for said
city and county during the forty-third and
forty-fourth fiscal years, out of the Income and
revenue of any succeeding year or years; pro-
vided, that any and all claims for making, re-
pairing, altering or for any work done upon or
for any material furnished for any street, lane,
alley, court, place or sidewalk, or for the con-
struction of any sewer or sewers in said city
and county are hereby excepted from the pro-
visions of this section; and in determining any
claim permitted to be paid by this section, no
statute of limitations shall apply in any man-
ner: and provided further, that the City of
Vallejo, in Solano County, may pay its extst-
ing indebtedness incurred in the construction
of its water works, v/her.ever two-thirds of the
electors thereof voting at an election held for
that purpose shall so decide. Any indebted-
ness or liability incurred contrary to this pro-
vision with the exception hereinbefore recited,
shall be void.
AMENDMENT NUMBER TWO.
(Being Senate Constitutional Amendment No.
10.)
A resolution proposing to the people of the
Btate of California an amendment to the Con-
stitution of the State, by adding a new sec-
tion, to be Unown anrt designated as Bection
Beve'n and one-half, article eleven thereof, pro-
viding for the framing by the inhabitants of
counties of local county government acts for
their own government. The said proposed new
section to read as follows:
Section 7H- The inhabitants of any county
may frame a county government act for their
own government, relating to the matters here-
inafter specified, and consistent with and sub-
ject to the Constitution and laws of this State,
by causing a board of fifteen freeholders, who
have been, for at leant five years; qualified
electors of such county, to be elected by the
qualified electors of such county, at any gen-
eral or special election, whose duty it shall be
within ninety days after such election, to pre-
pare and propose a county government act for
such county, which shall be signed in dupli-
cate by the members of such board, or a ma-
jority of them, and returned, one copy thereof
to thn Board of Supervisors or other legisla-
tive body of such county, and the other copy
to be sent to the Recorder of Deeds of the
county. Such proposed county government act
shall then be published in two papers of gen-
eral circulation in such county, or if there be
not two such papers, then in one only, for at
least twenty days, and within not less than
thirty days after such publication It shall be
submitted to the qualified electors of such
county, at a general or special election, and if
a majority of such Qualified electors voting
thereon shall ratify the same, It shall there-
after be submitted to the Legislature for its
rejection or approval, as a whole, without
power of alteration or amendment, and if ap-
proved by a majority of the members elected
to each house, it Bhall be the county govern-
ment act of such county, and shall in such
case become the organio law thereof and super-
sede any existing county government act, and
all amendments thereof, and all special laws
in-consistent with such county government act.
A copy of such county government act. certi-
fied by the President of the Board of Super-
visors or other legislative body of such county,
and authenticated by the seal of such county,
setting forth the submission of such county
government act to the electors, and its ratifica-
tion by them, shall be made in duplicate and
deposited, one in the office of the Secretary of
State the other, after being recorded in the
office' of the Recorder of Deeds in the county,
among the archives of the county.
All courts shall take Judicial nottde thereof.
The county government act so ratified may be
amended, at intervals of not less than two.
years by proposals therefor, submitted by the
legislative authority of the county, to the
qualified electors thereof, at a general or spe-
cial election held at least forty days after the
publication of such proposals for twenty days
in a newspaper of general circulation in such
county and ratified by at least three-fifths of
the qualified electors voting thereon, and ap-
proved by the Legislature aa herein provided
for the approval of the county government act.
In submitting any such county government act
any alternative article or proposition may be
presented for the choice of the voters, and may
be voted on separately without prejudice to
Â° It^han be competent in all county govern-
ment acts framed under the authority given by
this section, to provide for the manner in
which the time* at which, and the terms for
which the several township and county officers
other than Judges of the Superior Court, shall
be elected or appointed: for their compensa-
tion- for the number of such officers, for the
consolidation or segregation of offices, for the
number of deputies that each officer shall have,
and for the compensation payable, to each of
such deputies, for the manner in which, the
times in which, and the terms for which the
members of all boards of election shall be
elected or appointed and for the constitution,
regulation, compensation and government of
such boards, and of their cierks and attaches;
also to prescribe the manner and method by
which all elections by the people Bhall be con-
ducted' and may in addition determine the
tests and conditions upon which electors, po-
litical parties and organizations may partici-
pate in any primary election.
Whenever any county has. in the manner
and method herein pointed out. adopted any
eountv government act. and the FÂ«rai> shnll
have "been approved by the Legislature as
aforesaid, the direction of section* four and
five of this article providing for the uniformity
of a system of county governments throughout
the State and likewise providing for thÂ» elec-
tion and appointment of officers, and the regu-
lation of their compensation, shall not apply.
Said county government act shall, as to any of
the matters herelnabove provided for and de-
clared by such county government act, not be
subiect to any law or amendment enacted by
the Legislature, except by amendment first
submitted to the electors and ratified in the
manner hereinabove set forth.

Section 15. A Lieutenant Governor shall be
elected at the same time and place and In the
same manner as the Governor, and his term of
office and his qualifications shall be the same.
He shall be president of the Senate, but shall
only have a casting vote therein.
â  Section lÂ«. in case of the Impeachment of
the Governor, or his removal from office, death.
inability to discharge the powers and duties of
his office, resignation or absence, from the
State, the powers and duties of the office shall
devolve upon the Lieutenant Governor for the
residue of the term, or until the disability snail
cease.' And should the Lieutenant Governor be
Impeached, displaced, resign, die or become In-
capable of performing the duties of his office,
or be absent from the State,. the president pro
tempore of the Senate shall act as Governor un-
til the vacancy in the office of Governor shall
be filled at the next general election when
members of the Legislature shall be chosen, or
until such disability of the Lieutenant Gov-
ernor shall cease. In case of a vacancy in the
office of Governor for any of the reasons above
named, and neither the Lieutenant Governor
nor the president pro tempore of the Senate
shall succeed to the powers and duties of Gov-
ernor, then the powers and duties of such of-
fice . shall devolve upon the speaker of the As-
sembly, until ; the office of Governor shall be
filled at such general election. . .

TRANSPORTS NOW EXPECTED
THE ORDER OF DEPARTURE
NOT YET DETERMINED.
Colonel Smith of the Tennessee Regi
ment Tunis Over the Money
for the Thomas Fam
ily's Damages. .
, "There Is yet a possibility that the Sev
enth California Regiment may go to Ma
nila." . . â¢ . â¢
The above statement was made yester
day afternoon by Major General Merrlam
at department headquarters: - Beyond
this the general would not discuss the
matter or give out anything for publica
tion, but ho intimated that he had re
ceived news from Washington that
showed that the mustering out of the
Seventh may be ordered stopped. at any
moment, and in fact it is believed that
the orders have been received and will
be communicated to Captain Pratt to-day.
Late yesterday afternoon, when Cap
tain Pratt closed his office for. the day
and left for his honie at Fort Mason, he
declared that he had received no orders
to cease mustering put the. regiment, but
he stated that as a result of what he had
seen in the papers he had stopped giving
any of the men their discharges. The
captain- said that he had. found .the regi
ment in excellent shape' and that there
was no better In- the service. . As far as
Captain Pratt's work is concerned' he will
have, the regiment ready to go to Los An
geles to-morrow.
The transports are daily expected to be
gin arriving here. The Senator should
get here this week for a certainty, and
there may be two more vessels in. If It
is found that they do not have to go into
Urydock, then the llrst of â  the cle-tach
rm-nts of the troops may get away In
about two weeks' time. Quartermaster
Thompson had an interview with General
Merrlam, In which in>- latter stated that
the men must be supplied with ponchos
as they, had been found more service
able. than overcoats, for the latter had
proven of no use to the troops in the
warm climate. As to the order in which
the troops will leave no one at present
knows anything. It is probable that Gen
eral .Merrlam will ask General Miller for
his recommendations or he may turn the
whole matter over to him, which is more
probalile. â¢ . â¢

WANT TO GO HOME.
Soldiers of the Seventh Do Not
Want to Remain in the
Service:
It being almost a certainty that or
ders have been received here at depart
ment headquarters for the .retaining in
the service, of the Seventh Regiment, the
men of the organization have given ex
pression to their feelings in the matter.
They want to be mustered out. At least
50 per cent of them declare themselves as
anxious to leave the service.
When it became known yesterday that j
there was a possibility that the -regiment !
would not be â¢ mustered out, several of !
the companies held meetings and ap- |
pointed committees, who were instructed
to invoke the influence of their friends
and the press in having the regiment got-
ten out of the service as soon as possi
ble. The men declare that they do not
tvant to do garrison duty, and they bring
up the charge against their offirtM-s that
they have been working on General Mer- |
riam since his return to have the regi- I
ment retained simply because they want I
to continue to draw the salaries that !
they are getting now. Company B was
one of the companies- that appointed a
committee, and the members of it de
clare that positively 80 per cent of the
men want to get home and out of tho
service' at once. They say that even if.
they are the first to be sent to Manila
they still desire to leave the service.
They intend to use even* Influence in
jhelr power to be mustf-red out if it should
happen that orders have been received to
retain the regiment in service.
ARMY SUPPLY DEPOT.
The Largest ' West of Phila-
delphia Is Likely to Be
Established Here.
There are bright prospects that San
Francisco will have the largest depot for
&rmy supplies west of Philadelphia. The
quarters for supplies have already been
swelled by the addition of the old Ex
iminer building on Mission street. This
was secured for the purpose of storing
the arms, equipments, etc., released by
the Sixth and Seventh California regi
ments when mustered out.
Colonel Long, speaking of the matter at
the quartermaster's department yester
jlay, said: "I have no doubt that the
large increase to be made in- the forces
now stationed at various points in the
Pacific will cause a corresponding in
rrease in the capactlty of the army store
nouses in t...s city. It is very probable
that the department has under contem
plation the establishing of a very large
3epot for supplies in San Francisco. Thus
far I have received nothing official in the
matter."
It has been said that the building now
sccupied by Wells, Fargo & Co. will be
Utilized as soon as the company moves
to their new location.
tNGUGLIA'S CROSS COMPLAINT.
Charges His Wife With Having De
ceived and Defrauded Him.
â John B. Inguglia. the well-known mer
chant, whose wife a short time ago
Drought suit against him for divorce, al
leging infidelity as a cause of action.
Sled his answer and cross complaint yes
terday. Inguglia denies the allegations
t>f his wife, and in the cross complaint
tsks that the court set aside deeds to real
mate which he .alleges his wife procured
from him by fraud.
He states that immediately after his
Rife had procured the deeds her
Treatment of him changed. He then
law that her object in procuring the
Seeds was a mercenary one; she Intend
ed to leave him, and did not want to be
without funds. In substantiation of his
Jontentlbn he points to the divorce suit
recently filed by his wife.
In the prayer of the cross complaint
inguglia asks that his wife's suit be dis
missed and that the deeds be revoked.

est Shoot in Years Will Be

day.
Again will D. W. Mclaughlin be
crowned king of marksmen. For a year
he has reigned, and for the year to come
will the crack of his rifle emphasize hj s
claim to leadership of those who, of a fair
Sunday, go into the California Schuetzen
Club's range to burn powder and pile up
scores.
â¢ King McLaughliri's second claim to sov
ereignty was made good yesterday after
noon, when, at the end of the club's au-
McLaughlin.the New Shooting King.
tumnal shoot, it was announced that his
score was best of the spven marksmen
who entered in the king's tourney. For a
good half hour the racket of rifles was
quieted, and the popping of champagne
corks, that had only the celling for a
target, gave the noisy eclat that Is char
acteristic of all Schuetzen meets. It was
a gala Ume, this fending ot the king, and
only Philo Jacoby's enforced absence
dimmed the ardor r>f it.
Outside the king list, D. B. Faktor cap
tured first honors and will take all of the

John I'tschig Sr. and L. C. Babin, all

. marks finale at the end of the season's
festivity. There are the prizf-s vet to be

jolly ceremony that always attends upon
the presentation Sunday is the day set.
Captain Fred' W. Schumann is arranging
for the event, which will be held at the
park, near San Rafael.
â¢ The morning will be given over to work
in the ranges and picnicking through the
grounds; lunch at noon and. the exercises
in the pavilion after it. The pretty cere-,
mony of the annual passing o. the" loving
cup will follow the distribution of the
prizes, and it is counted no small honor
to drink from that same loving cup for it
is the only idol that good Schuetzens bow
down to, and most r>f those of the old
Centennial team, who .by their prowess
brought i.t back from Philadelphia in 1876
Will be at hand to have their sip.

A DEATH-BED MARRIAGE.
Suit was filed yesterday by Malvlna De
visnes to set aside a decree of distribu
tion in the estate of Henri Devisnes,
who died November 27, 1896. The plain
tiff alleges that she is the widow of the
deceased, having married him in FrancÂ©
In June of ISSO. In consequence she de
mands distribution to her of the entire
estate.
The complaint is directed against the
estate of Vlctorine Augustine Devisnes,
to whom the estate of the deceased was
bequeathed. It is averred that Mrs. Vic
torine Devisnes was formerly Vlctorinfe
Augustine Estennvin. and that she wed
ded Devisnes under that name while he
was on his dfathbed, well knowing that
the dying man had a wife living. The
plaintiff holds that she is entitled to the
estate, which is valued at $2175, and asks
judgment accordingly.
Nicaraguan Canal Congress.
Since the proposition to hold a Nicara
guan canal congress was llrst suggested
the greatest of Interest has been mani
fest by people from all parts of the coun
try. This congress is to be held in Chi
cago on November 15. 16 and 17. Its pur
pose Is to secure favorable legislation for
the big waterway between the two oceans
and for its construction, operation and
control by the United States. The call
for this meeting was made by a combina
tion of the Chicago and San Â£>iego Cham
bers of Commerce and the American
Nicaraguan Canal Association. Invita
tions were sent to the President of tue
I nlted States, and State, county and mu
nicipal officers, and to the presidents of
all commercial bodies, to send representa
tives. None of these bodies have been
more interested in the project than the
local Chamber of Commerce. Now comes
Santa Barbara, one of Its influential citi
zens writing to Secretary Scott that tne
people of that county are becoming In
terested and desire to be represented in
the coming meeting in Chicago
