THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, SEPTEMBER 27, 1898.
THE FRAWLEYS
IN COSTUME
Miss Bates Scores in "An
Enemy to the King."
THE O'NEIL'S PARTHENIA
"ROMEO AND JULIET" GIVEN
AGAIN AT THE TIVOLI.
Alcazar's "In Mizzoura" â Morosco's
"Trilby"â Orpheum ana Al
liambva Variety â Con
tinuation of "Jones."
Mr. Frawley and his company dared the
superstitions last night by moving over
the way to the Baldwin. Superstition, or,
rather, the superstitious defiance of ordin
ary superstition, has had a lot to do wka
the success of this companyâ at least so
the press agent would have us believe.
When the Frawleys first came to San
Francisco and opened at the Columbia
the press agent had worlds of fun and ad
vertising out of Mr. Frawley's dare-devil
fondness for the number "thirteen." On
the opening night there were thirteen ac
tors in the cast, thirteen lights in the lod
by, thirteen precious stones In Mr. Fried
lander's shirt toilet, and who remem
bers what other mystic combinations!
And the season began promptly on the
thirteenth of the month. Mr. Frawley, I
have, read somewhere, didn't want to
change his theater this time, but the Co
lumbia bookings were full, and the Fraw
leys were needed to make out the Baldwin
season, so he was "Induced," and Miss
Blanche Bates, the flower of the old band,
was borrowed from Augustin Daly to help
along the occasion. And as many of the
old superstitions as possible were put in
force too. In fact, the fhirteens were
doubled on, and last night saw twenty
six actors in the cast and an opening on
the twenty-sixth of the month.
The piece was "An Enemy to the King,"
a costumed romance by R. N. Stephens, ,'
that was made successful in New York !
by E. H. Sothern's work in the leading
! part. You have read before now of Mr.
Frawley's loss of a loading man. It was
owing to that disaster that he appeared
last night in the role made famous by I
Mr. Sothern. Any one that has studied
Mr. Sothern and Mr. Frawley will admit
that their methods and personalities are
| somewhat different. To me it is even
I strange that they should have an occu
pation in common. But Mr. Frawley
will act; and last nigfrt as Ernanton de
Launay, the Hugeonot â¢hero of Mr. Ste
phen's romance, he acted for his life, one
might say. Mr. Frawley's acting is of
known color and quality, and it never
changes. In a certain line of comically
stiffened parts, like say the fcstina lente
officer in "The Senator," he is as much j
as admirable and delightfully funny. In \
parts of heroic character he is perhaps j
Just as funny but not so admirable. So
there is no need to describe his effort of j
last night. He was Mr. Frawleyâ that
and nothing else in the world.
But for all Mr. Frawley's unconvlction
as a serious character there is something
in the man himself that is earnest and
good-natured and makes friends in the
audience; and this quality, whatever it
may be, helped him better than ever last
night. He was recalled at the close of
the excellent third act, and made an hon
est little speech, straight from the heart,
in which he said that short of the small
pox the Frawley Company had suffered
every epidemic in nature this season;
that it was due more to some belated
i and inexplicable piece of luck than to
| good management that we found its rem
j nants here at all, that he had taken this
I his first costume part in an emergency
and at the last moment, that he hardly
i knew the lines, and feared he wronged
| the author, but that he thanked us and
i hoped to do better later in the week.
It was a genuine little talk, and cheered
us all up an<l covered a multitude of
artistic sins.
The entfre company seemed more, or less
nervous, but nevertheless the play was
given a successful trying out and the
j prospect is a performance with several
i examples of good acting within a night or
I two. Already Miss Bates has mastered
i her part of Julie de Vernon. It is not
i the best she has ever done, but it is fine.
| distinguished work, never overdrawn and
i always warm and fragrant with woman
liness. She was cheered and flowered at
the end of the first act until the real
I tears were in her eyes. She came down
!to the lights and said: "All I can say
I Is that 1 thank you from the bottom of
1 my heart for this kind welcome horne â
. for It is home, although it isn't the Col
umbia Theater." And everybody was sin
' cerely glad to have her back again. It is
I no longer mere provincial pride to reckon
j Blanche Bates among the foremost ac
1 tresses of America.
Sam Edwards as the serio-comic hench
! man of the Huguenot hero was consplcu
i ous among the others of the cast that
i did well; Mr. Roberts was proud and
, splendid as the Catholic Governor; Joseph
i P. Keeefe celebrated hie first appearance
with the company by a very plausible
i soldier of fortune, and G. Foster Platt
| was fairly the same in a similar part.
' Miss Georgia Wells hadn't very much to
i do as the innkeeper's daughter, but did it
I nicely enough. The same for Miss Elea
| nor Carey as the Innkeeper's wife.
As to the play itself it fs a costume
drama (a type not to be taken too serious-
I ly) with a really beautiful third act. It
ris no more like a comic opera than most
j of its kind; maybe not so much. It is pic
! torial to a degree, not riotously florid in
; the speeches, and not overloaded with
"situations." In motive it is the same as
\ "I'nder the Red Robe"â only this time it
lis a woman, not a man, whose "honor
j rooted in dishonor stood." The life of
' Julie's father is at stake for having
sheltered Huguenots, and the price she
â  must pay to save it Is to decoy a young
! Huguenot captain into the hands of his
i enemies. He saves her from Insult in the
i first act, and tries vigorously to give up
his life to save her again in the third. And
she does about what Gil de Berault would
ido in similar circumstances. There is a
plenty of high words and tall fighting and
considerable pictorial charm; and. as I said
[ earlier, the third act is beautiful. In It
sentiment and heroics are skillfully
blended, and the action is lithe and tell
ing. This is the sun-dial act that you see
Pictured on the posters, and it deserves its
ume. The scenery Is well-meant and
i lively to look at, and the Frawleys quite
: credible In their sixteenth-century
i clothes. ASHTON STEVENS.
Columbia.
The week began at the Columbia with the
I Greek love idyl "Ingomar, the Barbarian."
I ami Nance O'Neill In a decollete Del Sarte
j role with Edwin Forrest trimmings. Although
i suffering from hoarseness Miss O'Neill acted
| the trying part In a manner which gained for
I her the sympathy of her audience. The Greek
! dress gave her ample opportunity to display a
! line pair of arms and a bust fair enough to
! satisfy a sculptor, while her lines calling for
i tenderness and maidenly devotion were spoken
! for the most part In the fair-weather way
| called for by the spirit of the play. Here, It
must be said. Miss O'Neill was at her best.
I the fault of overdoing so conspicuous In her
acting In other plays being absent In this. At
times there was a little staglness In the effect,
a touch of elocution on stilts, but those times
were not fre<juent enough to mar the effect
of an otherwise good performance.
Herbert Carr made a stalwart enough Ingo
mar and delivered the rounded periods and
faultless rhetoric of the barbarian with a fine
voice, a splendid pair of legs and a magnificent
physique aiding In establishing him as a fa
vorite with more ladies than Parthenia. L.
W'r>od as Father Myron was funny In his ear
nestness and Mr. Weaver as Polvdor was re
pulsive enough in action and make-up to suit
the most exacting of gallery gods, or Greek
gods for the matter of that. "Ingomar" will
be repeated this evening, to-morrow evening
and at the Saturday matinee.
The Alcazar:
"In Mlxzoura" opened at the Alcazar Theater
last evei.iig for the first time in this city a*
po|)ulnr prices. It was a success In every par
ticular, staging, cast and execution. The
scenery was from the brush of Edward Will
iams and was so true to life, Missouri life
that even the old split rail fence appears to ex
tend for a mile up the turnpike road. Those
nho have seen "In Mlizoura" will remember
the plot, of how the old blacksmith's pretty
Bemlnary educated daughter falls in love with
a polished train robber and casts aside the
love of a rough but true friend, the County
Bheriff, who. through his regard for the girl,
allows the fellow to escape, only to meet his
death In another county. The. part of the old
blacksmith, Joe Vernon, was taken by George
Osbourne, who showed that he comprehended
the finer parts of his work. Gretchen Lyons
made a very pretty Kate Vernon and though
taking a leading part, she was obliged to share
the honors with her "Sister 'Lizabeth" Ver
non, Joe's youngest daughter, 'inls difficult
part was assumed by Miss Maybell Bowman,
a girl scarce! j- 15 years of age.
Seven years ago the child made her debut
on the Alcazar stage in some small part. Last
evening she received an ovation that many
an older actress would have highly appre
ciated. At the end of the first act, after a
wreath of beautiful floral offerings had been
presented to her, the audience called loudly
time and again for the little miss and would
not be satisfied until she appeared before them
"all by her lonely." The requirements of her
part make it necessary that the actress should
be green, raw, awkwardâ in fact, a typical lit
tle Piker. Mlsa Bowman was all of these. She
is a whole show in herself in this class of
work.
Ernest Hastings, as "Jim" Radburn, the
Sheriff, was well appreciated. He understands
and acts the part well. Clarence Montaine
as Dave, the blacksmith's helper, made much
laughter by his awkwardness and comical ap
pearance. Howard Scott made a good rascal
in the person of Robert Travers. Miss Marie
Howe as Joe's wife delineates the character
as though she had been born and "riz" in
Missouri. The others were acceptable.
The Orpheum.
The Orpheum'B new bill is fully up to the
standard. Of the four new acts there is not a
weak one in the lot. The clever little comedy
"The Man Up Btairs" was presented by Mr.
and Mrs. R. J. Dustan, and all the parts were
ably handled. As the Irish servant girl Miss
Maggie Weston was exceedingly funny. In fact
her Irishisms and mishaps tended to divert
attention from the more delicate comedy work
of Mr. and Mrs. Dustln, who In their way may
be classed among the best that have appeared
on the Orpheum stage. Lew Hawkins was a
favorite from the start. He returns with a lot
of new material which he delivers in catching
style
The Anglo-American quartet in a military
sketch entitled "In Camp" introduce some
gongs that are well received. Sankey Brothers
class as Al acrobats. They perform all their
work with an ease and finish that gives them
place among the very best. Their balancing
is particularly clever. Louis Cazeau In some
new feats of magic. Mile. Franko the vtolln
lste. Bessie Bonehlll, the two Paolis and dog
and the blogranh with new views round out
one of the Orpheum'B best bills.
Grand Opera- House,
"Trilby" as presented at the Grand Opera
house last night could not be called a success.
The stock company, although reinforced, did
not rise equal to the occasion, with the excep
tion of Leslie Morosco as Zoo Zoo and Lorena
Atwood as Trilby, who gave an artistic inter
pretation of their parts. James M. Brophy
made hie reappearance in "his own conception"
of Svengali, but he cannot be congratulated on
the result as it is not by any means an im
provement upon the conceptions of Wilton
Lackaye, Theodore Roberts or Frederick
Pauldlng.
The Alhambra.
Nat M. Brigham, the American ballad tenor,
was the principal new attraction at the Al
hnmbra last night. He has a clear resonant
voice, and enunciates well, but there is not
much expression in his singing. He took well
with the audience, and was encore.l three times.
Mr. and Mrs. Sydney iJrew in "Diana on the
Chase" made the hit of the evening. The
Austin sisters in their aerial acts were partic
ularly good, and Corlnne as usual captured
everybody hy her singing and playing on the
mandolin. The Alhambra has a good pro
gramme this week.
The Tivoll.
Gounod's lyrical masterpiece attracted a good
audience to the Tivoll last night. It seems to
me that there Is hardly another opera In which
the. character and the sentimentality of the
composer Is better reflected. Gounod's mar
velously composed music stands upon a pure
and platonic plane. Like Chopin, Grieg. Wajr
ner and perhaps Salnt-Saens, he sets his Indi
vidual seal upon his work in a way that can
scarcely be. mistaken. But Gounod, as an ex
ponent of pure music, untlngedâ l will not say
tainted â by mundane considerations, seemed to
swim aloft with the eternals, ajid to view mere
human praise or blame with the fine indiffer
ence of Olympian Jove.
The performance was given in excellent style.
It' is almost bordering on over-criticism to
point to fault at all, but if one may be hinted
at it is that the conception of Signor Zerni is
entirely in the one key of indifference. How
ever, his rendering of the beautiful duo won
him well deserved applause. Miss Lichter is
a sweet Juliet. She shows " this charming
flower of girlhood as It ought to be shown, and
she certainly has studied this congenial role
with dauntless zeal. Nothing more Impressive
has ever been shown at the Tlvoll than the fa
mous balcony scene. Miss Llchter â¢ has ; very
mobile features. This Is. the perfection of real
art, where it is not always the voice alone and
where acting has its due weight and Its proper
share. - Her -attitudes -suggest thought and In
the Intensity .of their, conviction the eyes
Inferred an : atmosphere which by no great
stretch of the Imagination recalled the aureole
of the painter. 1 '
The breezy Mr. Pruette helped very much
to make this opera a success. Would â â¢ that
space permitted me to give a more detailed ac
count of each artist's talented performance,
but I will not fall to praise the splendid work
of the chorus. "Romeo and Juliet" was cer
tainly a success. _21 G. H. MORRE.
Chutes Theater.
Pletro Marino, who graduated at the Brussels
Conservatory of Music last year, and who was
Tsaye's pride and favorite pupil, played at the
Chutes Free Theater last evening and scored a
hit. After popular and classical selections he
gave the intermezzo from "Cavalleria Rusti
cana," and was even then compelled to respond
to another encore. The rest of the bill is un
usually good.
Olympia.
At the Olympia Music Hall last night Lillian
Monteray, a descriptive singer, received several
recalls and Nadine Allen, the chic soubrette,
made a great hit. Kelly and Vlolette can be
classed as among the best. Madeline, the con
tortion dancer, Martin's dogs and some five or
six other acts round out a good variety bill.
Lely's Song Recital.
An artistic and unique pong recital was given
in Sherman &â¢ Clay Hull last night by Durward
Lely. who used to be Pattl's tenor. Mr. Lely's
selections ranged from Schubert to Sullivan,
and he snng all the songs with charming dis
cretion and taste, bringing out the full strength
of the text as well as the muÃic. Mrs. Lely
accompanied and played a Beethoven sonata
and a Schubert Impromptu. A second recital
will be given to-night. and another on Saturday
night in the Fame hall.
New Comedy.
Harry Corson Clarke and his bright little
company will continue all of this week at the
New Comedy Theater in "What Happened to
Jones." There will be a special profession!! !
matinee Wednesday and a souvenir matinee
Saturday. At the latter Mr. Clarke will hold
a reception on the stage and present to every
lady present a real fan. Miss Lillian Young
will appear Thursday night In the curtain
raiser, "One Touch of Nature," alternating
the balance of the week 'with Miss Olive Snider.

OUT FOB A LARK.
Jennie Wallace Arrested for Mas-
querading in a Soldier's Uniform.
Jennie Wallace, a handsome young
woman, was arrested by Officer Sylvester
at an early hour yesterday morning while
entering 211 O'Farrell street, for masquer
ading in male attire.
Jennie Is a friend of some of the Ten
nessee soldiers, and several of them
called on her Sunday night. For a lark
she was Induced to don the uniform' of
Private E. M. Higgins of Company A,
and, accompanied by three soldiers, she
went to a restaurant on Eddy street to
have supper, Hlggins being left behind,
as he did not care to rig himself out in
Jennie's clothes.
They were returning from supper when
Sylvester thought there was something
unusual in Jennie's carriage for a man,
and as they were entering the house he
stopped them and Jennie admitted her
sex. She was at once placed under ar
rest.
Friends brought her clothes to the pris
on yesterday morning and Hlggins' uni
form was placed in charge of the prop
erty clerk. When the case was called In
Judge Low's court Higgins' predicament
was explained to the Judge, and he made
an order on the property clerk for the
delivery of the uniform to Hlggins' com
panions, and continued the hearing of the
charge against Jennie until this morn
ing.

Secretary Martin Does Not Consider

hibition.
Artists are rowing: with one another.
The. regular fall exhibition of the Art In
stitute opens on' October 13, ana ievery
where that the. knights of the brush meet
protests are heard and veiled flings at the
"jury of acceptance or rejection" are mer
rily tossed to and fro.
The ladles also have chosen to take part
In the general dissatisfaction, and after
noon studio teas are given for the sole
ana only purpose .of discussing whether
:lt is possible for a woman â on a hang
ing committeeâto act fairly and squarely
toward another voman.
The dissension in the ranks of art is not
a' matter of a day nor a week. Those in
terested in. the fight claim it is the natural
outcome of many years of autocratic op
pression, of worthy pictures of unknown
struggling artists relegated to the "sky"
and above all to the bugaboo of all art
istsâbad light; Favorite pupils, so thÂ«
grumblers claim, find their pictures on the
line, while the .outsider has his picturo
hung in some obseuro or out-of-the-way
place. . â 
The especial "kick" of the lady exhlolt
ors this year is that Mrs. jdce B. Chitten
den Is on .the hanging committee and a
woman, they claim, is never, can never
end will never be just to another woman.
There .is also sunie talk that October is
too early for a profitable resulting exhibi
tion, and that nearer Christmas time
might find more purchasers. The dissat
isfied ones, moreover, hold that the studio
tea and exhibition give the artist in every
way a better chance, both as to showing
and disposing of his work.
Up at the institute they only laugh at
all the reports of dissatisfaction, and Sec
retary Martin sums up the authors of all
the charges under the head of "kickers."
"It la the kickers," said Martin.
"Every exhibition brings piem out with a
long string of grievances, a dr-nunciation
of the institute and a determination not
to exhibit. Hut they all calm down, send
In their pictures, are delighted to have
them accepted and hung as best the com
mittee sees flt. â¢
"All cannot be hung on the line, and all
cannot have just the light that each art
ist demands. God's own sunlight cannot
1 c t'-mpered to please some exhibitors.
for they naturally must be dissatisfied.
l!i;rn kickers, that's the whole thing."
The charge against Mrs. Chittenden is
scorned by the g*-nial secretary.
"Mrs. Chittenden is justice personified,
and bo gentleâ why, she would not harm a
By." â¢ .
â  Martin's only trouble in the midst of
nl! this turmoil is the probability that
the night class on account of its lack of
pupils will have to he discontinued.

LEFT ALONE TO SUFFEB.
Young Girl Found In a Lodging-
House in a Precarious Condition.
Neille'KaUy, 18 years of age, was found
alone and sick In a room In the Gerard
House, W2- Seventh street, yesterday after
noon. She was brought there a week
ago by a young man who gave his name
as Madden, and has had no attention
since that time.
Dr. Bunnell -was called In and found
she was suffering from pneumonia. Of
ficer McMurray had her removed to the
City and County Hospital.
Health Officials May Go.
There was an informal caucus of sev
eral members of the Board of Health
held last evening at the office of Dr. Wil
liamson., to discuss retrenchment in ex
penditures. The decision has been reached,
however, that If the worst comes, four
or five milk inspectors and a number of
others In the pure food department will
have to go. The appearance of Texas
fever among cattle in Solano County was
considered by the doctors Lost evening,
and at the board meeting to-night steps
will be taken to prevent the Introduction
c>i diseased meat into this market.

While he flatly denies all charges made

unteer any Information. He was married
about two years ago by Justice Carroll,
but has not lived regularly with his wife,
who, he claims, dissipated freely. The
last time he admits having seen her was
on the afternoon of the 18th at her room,
1317 Powell street. She was sick at the
time, but had plenty to eat.
The next he heard of her was when
the papers published her story. He then
went to the house of a friend, where ho
has been hiding ever since.
"I was married two years ago," he said

wife. We quarreled at times, but I never

KARSSA 'S PRETTY DAUGHTER.
The Old Syrian Fears She Will Be
Abducted.
The old trouble in the Syrian colony
has Ftarted out afresh, and yesterday
Joseph Rahy was arrested on complaint
of Abdullah Karssa on the charge of dis
turbing the peace. HÂ© was released on
bonds.
Karssa has a pretty daughter, 18 years
of age, and when swearing to the com
plaint he said that Rahy and others were
trying to abduct the girl. Sunday he met
Rahy on Second and Natoma streets, near
his house, and Rahy, after abusing him,
put his hand in his hip pocket as if to
draw a revolver. â¢
Rahy denied making the threatening
motion, but admitted that he had some
hot words with Karssa, as Karssa had
been calling him offensive names and he
dared the old man to fight him.
"The fact is," said Rahy. "the girl
wants to get married, but her father is
opposed to it, as ue wants h^r to make
money for him by going into the coun
try peddling his wares. That Is the
whole cause of the trouble. I have known
her since we were children tog-ether, and
she looks upon me as a brother. The idea
of any one wanting to abduct her Is ab
surd."
TTpset the Testimony.
The Supreme Court has reversed the
Judgment of the lower court of Riverside
County in the case of John F. Milner, con
victed of killing S. J. Darrah, on the
ground that the court erred in admitting
certain testimony of n physician, the tes
timony being more expert than relating
to facts.
Darrah sold some land to Milner, the
Hollar reservin" water rights and certain
rights of way over the land. These con
sidrrations were the cause of several dis
putes, resulting in Milner using a shotgun
on Darrah with deadly effect.
In the Divorce Courts.
L.lla Brennan has been granted a di
vorce from Daniel J. Brennan on the
ground of cruelty. Mrs. Brennan was
given alimony in the sum of $20 monthly.
Margaret M. Huron has sued William F.
Baron for a divorce, alleging failure to
provide as a cause of action.
Willie Lynch Better.
The condition of little Willie Lynch, al
leged to have been injured by Policeman
Kenville on Friday evening, has improved
and there is a chance of his ultimate re
covery. The polico have collected a num
bor or statements of eye-witnesses of the
affair.
A HOT TIME
EXISTS AMONG
POLICE JUDGES
Low and Mogan Have
a Very Lively Tilt.
EACH CRITICIZES THE OTHER
CAUSE OF TROUBLE, REFUSAL
TO ISSUE WARRANTS.
The Grand Jury May Be Asked to
Straighten Matters Out and
Stop the Annoyance to
the Public.
The atmosphere around the Police
Courts Is surcharged with war spirit, and
at any moment there may be an explo
sion that will have dire results to the
Judges.
There has been friction among the offi
cials for some time as to the issuing of
warrants. Judge Mogan has had more
than his share of them, and the refusal
Saturday to sign the warrant in the Falk
diseased meat case was owing to that
fact. Judge Joachimsen was absent yes
terday, and as neither Judge Low nor
Judge Conlan would take the case Mo
gan agreed to do so.
Yesterday Joseph Platz, a plumber at j
262 a Pine street, applied to Assistant Pros- [
ecuting Attorney Mann of Judge Mogan's !
court for the arrest of some boys who !
had annoyed him by ringing his door bell
and breaking his windows. Mann, after â 
listening to Platz' story, declined to is
sue a warrant, as in his opinion no crime ;
had been committed, and he did not be- 'â â 
lieve in arresting small boys anyhow on
such frivolous charges.
Platz then went to Judge Low and
when the Judge learned that Mann had
refused to issue the warrant he advised [
Platz to take the matter b^ore the Grand
Jury, and he would assist him all in his ;
power.
"it Is time this thing was stopped," said |
the Judge. "Mann tanes all the big cases |
and when a paltry case comes up he
shoves it upon me. Judge Mogan sits
swelling his chest out as he surveys his
crowded court because he gets the impor
tant cases. Ills warrant clerk has no I
right to be in my clerk's oftice anyhow.
He was sent there so as to allow Judge
Mogan to have chambers. I am a Judge
much longer than he and I have to be
without cnambers."
Judge Mogan was very much surprised
when informed of Judge L/OW's outburst,
saying:
"It ill becomes one Judge to criticize
the other in his absence, i suppose that
Judge .Low sees defeat staring him in the i
face at the coming election and that is
the only reason why he should go out of ;
his way to throw a slur at me. If he and
the other Judges would do as much work
as I, they would have some reason to i
complain. 1 am here from ten in the â 
morning till late every' afternoon and
have had some night sessions. I have a
Jury trial every afternoon till October 3 |
and my calendar is full with other cases.
I am a public servant, sworn to do my i
duty and It la to the public I shall look
for a criticism of my work and not to
Judge Low."
Almost every day men have to go
from court to court asking for a warrant
and unless it is stopped the Grand Jury
will be appealed, to and. the blame placed
upon the proper shoulders.
WANT MOPS AND PRUNES.
Germany's Crops Have Failed and
California's Are in Demand.
J. A. Fllcher, manager of the State â 
Board of Trade, has received a letter I
from one of his correspondents, Carl Old
ach, of Hamburg, Germany, which con
tains considerable information that Cali
fornia farmers may well heed. Mr. Old
ach states that the prune crop of nearly
every district in Germany is a practical
failure. The California drjed prune is in
great demand and the prices are good.
He states that if the surplus of the crop
of this State is sent to Kurope he is
sure that the producers will be well re- j
warded.
The writer gives the details and ruling '
prices, which show that this kind or
fruit is almost a luxury in the realm of
the Kaiser. As Mr. Oldach is in the com
mission business and desires to Increase
his California business, Mr. Filcher places
the greatest confidence in what he says.
The same writer also gives valuable in- i
formation as to the condition of the hop j
production in Germany. He says that,
like the prunes the crop in nearly every
district in the German empire has proved
to be a failure. His remarks lead to the
belief that Germany alone would be a fine
market for all of this product that the
States of California, Oregon and Wash
ington could spare.
